## Chapter: Wireless Network Standards

### Introduction

Wireless communication has become an integral part of our daily lives, and the need for efficient and reliable wireless networks is more pressing than ever. This chapter, "Wireless Network Standards," delves into the principles and standards that govern the operation of wireless networks. 

Wireless networks, also known as wireless local area networks (WLANs), are designed to provide wireless access to computer networks. They are used in a variety of settings, from home networks to large corporate networks. The IEEE 802.11 network standards, also known as Wi-Fi, are the most widely used wireless network standards. These standards define the specifications for implementing wireless local area networks (WLANs).

In this chapter, we will explore the various aspects of wireless network standards, including the IEEE 802.11 standards, the protocols they use, and the challenges they face. We will also discuss the role of these standards in the broader context of wireless communication, and how they interact with other wireless technologies.

The chapter will also delve into the principles behind wireless network standards, including the underlying technologies and concepts. This will include a discussion on the principles of wireless communication, the role of protocols in wireless networks, and the challenges faced in implementing these standards.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a comprehensive understanding of wireless network standards, their principles, and their role in wireless communication. This knowledge will be invaluable for anyone working in the field of wireless communication, whether as a network administrator, a network engineer, or a researcher in the field.




#### 1.3d Multipath in Underwater Communications

Underwater communication is a challenging field due to the unique characteristics of the underwater environment. The propagation of electromagnetic waves in water is significantly different from that in air, and this difference is further amplified in the underwater environment. In this section, we will explore the characteristics of multipath propagation in underwater communications and its implications for wireless communication systems.

#### 1.3d.1 Multipath Propagation in Underwater Communications

Multipath propagation in underwater communications is a result of the complex interaction between the transmitted signal and the underwater environment. The signal can reach the receiver via multiple paths, each with different delays and attenuations. This can cause the received signal to be a combination of multiple copies of the transmitted signal, each delayed and attenuated differently.

The underwater environment is typically characterized by a high density of reflective and diffractive objects, such as marine life, underwater structures, and underwater vehicles. These objects can cause the signal to be reflected, diffracted, and scattered in various directions. The signal can also penetrate through these objects, leading to additional paths and further complicating the propagation conditions.

#### 1.3d.2 Characteristics of Multipath Propagation in Underwater Communications

The characteristics of multipath propagation in underwater communications can be described by the multipath channel model. This model represents the channel as a superposition of multiple paths, each with its own delay, attenuation, and phase shift. The total received signal is the sum of the signals from all paths.

The delay spread, which is the difference in arrival times of the multipath components at the receiver, can be significant in underwater communications due to the complex propagation paths. This can cause intersymbol interference (ISI), which is the overlapping of adjacent symbols. However, the high density of reflective and diffractive objects in underwater environments can lead to a larger number of multipath components, which can increase the channel capacity and improve the reliability of the communication system.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the techniques used to mitigate the effects of multipath propagation in underwater communications.




#### 1.4a MIMO in Urban Environments

Multiple-Input Multiple-Output (MIMO) technology has been widely adopted in wireless communication systems due to its ability to significantly increase data throughput. In urban environments, where the propagation conditions are often complex and challenging, MIMO technology can be particularly beneficial.

#### 1.4a.1 MIMO in Urban Environments

Urban environments are characterized by a high density of buildings, structures, and other obstacles that can cause significant multipath propagation. This means that a transmitted signal can reach the receiver via multiple paths, each with different delays and attenuations. MIMO technology can exploit this multipath propagation to increase data throughput.

The MIMO technology uses multiple antennas at both the transmitter and receiver to coherently resolve more information than possible using a single antenna. This is achieved through Spatial Division Multiplexing (SDM), which spatially multiplexes multiple independent data streams, transferred simultaneously within one spectral channel of bandwidth. Each spatial stream requires a discrete antenna at both the transmitter and the receiver.

#### 1.4a.2 MIMO in Urban Environments: Challenges and Solutions

Despite its potential benefits, implementing MIMO in urban environments can be challenging due to the high cost and complexity of the MIMO architecture. Each additional antenna at the transmitter and receiver requires a separate radio-frequency chain and analog-to-digital converter, making it more expensive to implement than non-MIMO systems.

To address this challenge, researchers have proposed various solutions. One approach is to use beamforming techniques to focus the signal towards the receiver, reducing the need for multiple antennas. Another approach is to use adaptive precoding and postcoding techniques to improve the signal quality and reduce the complexity of the MIMO architecture.

#### 1.4a.3 MIMO in Urban Environments: Future Trends

As the demand for wireless data continues to grow, the use of MIMO technology in urban environments is expected to increase. Future trends in MIMO technology include the development of more efficient and cost-effective MIMO architectures, as well as the integration of MIMO with other advanced technologies such as cognitive radio and software-defined radio.

In conclusion, MIMO technology offers significant potential for improving wireless communication in urban environments. Despite the challenges, the benefits of MIMO make it a key technology for the future of wireless communications.




#### 1.4b MIMO in Indoor Environments

Indoor environments present a unique set of challenges for wireless communication systems. The propagation conditions are often complex and unpredictable, with a high degree of multipath propagation due to the presence of furniture, walls, and other obstacles. This can make it difficult to maintain a reliable wireless connection, especially in large buildings or complex structures.

#### 1.4b.1 MIMO in Indoor Environments

Multiple-Input Multiple-Output (MIMO) technology can be particularly beneficial in indoor environments. By using multiple antennas at both the transmitter and receiver, MIMO can exploit the multipath propagation to increase data throughput and improve the reliability of the wireless connection.

The MIMO technology uses Spatial Division Multiplexing (SDM) to transmit multiple data streams simultaneously over the same frequency band. This is achieved by using multiple antennas at both the transmitter and receiver, each of which can transmit or receive a different data stream. This allows for a higher data rate and improved reliability, especially in environments with high levels of multipath propagation.

#### 1.4b.2 MIMO in Indoor Environments: Challenges and Solutions

Despite its potential benefits, implementing MIMO in indoor environments can be challenging due to the high cost and complexity of the MIMO architecture. Each additional antenna at the transmitter and receiver requires a separate radio-frequency chain and analog-to-digital converter, making it more expensive to implement than non-MIMO systems.

To address this challenge, researchers have proposed various solutions. One approach is to use beamforming techniques to focus the signal towards the receiver, reducing the need for multiple antennas. Another approach is to use adaptive precoding and postcoding techniques to improve the signal quality and reduce the complexity of the MIMO architecture.

#### 1.4b.3 MIMO in Indoor Environments: Future Trends

As wireless communication continues to evolve, the use of MIMO technology in indoor environments is expected to increase. With the advent of 5G technology, MIMO is expected to play a crucial role in achieving the high data rates and reliability required for applications such as virtual reality and autonomous vehicles.

In addition, the development of new antenna technologies, such as metamaterials and reconfigurable antennas, could further reduce the cost and complexity of MIMO systems, making them more accessible for indoor applications.

#### 1.4b.4 MIMO in Indoor Environments: Conclusion

In conclusion, MIMO technology offers significant benefits for wireless communication in indoor environments. Despite the challenges associated with its implementation, the potential gains make it a promising technology for the future of wireless communication. As research continues to advance, we can expect to see more innovative solutions to the challenges of implementing MIMO in indoor environments.




#### 1.4c MIMO in Rural Environments

Rural environments present a unique set of challenges for wireless communication systems. The propagation conditions are often complex and unpredictable, with a high degree of multipath propagation due to the presence of various obstacles such as mountains, valleys, and vegetation. This can make it difficult to maintain a reliable wireless connection, especially in areas with large distances between the transmitter and receiver.

#### 1.4c.1 MIMO in Rural Environments

The MIMO technology uses Spatial Division Multiplexing (SDM) to transmit multiple data streams simultaneously over the same frequency band. This is achieved by using multiple antennas at both the transmitter and receiver, each of which can transmit or receive a different data stream. To address this challenge, researchers have proposed various solutions. One approach is to use beamforming techniques to focus the signal towards the receiver, reducing the need for multiple antennas. Another approach is to use adaptive precoding and postcoding techniques to improve the signal quality and reduce the complexity of the MIMO architecture.

#### 1.4c.3 MIMO in Rural Environments: Future Trends

As technology continues to advance, the future of MIMO in rural environments looks promising. Researchers are exploring new techniques to reduce the complexity and cost of MIMO systems, making them more accessible for use in rural areas. Additionally, advancements in digital signal processing and hardware technology are expected to improve the performance of MIMO systems, making them more reliable and efficient in rural environments.

Furthermore, the integration of MIMO with other emerging technologies, such as cognitive radio and software-defined radio, could provide additional benefits in rural environments. These technologies could help mitigate the effects of interference and improve the utilization of the available spectrum, making wireless communication more reliable and efficient in rural areas.

In conclusion, MIMO technology has the potential to significantly improve wireless communication in rural environments. While there are challenges to its implementation, ongoing research and advancements in technology are expected to overcome these challenges and make MIMO a viable solution for rural wireless communication.

Understanding the principles behind these networks is crucial for anyone working in the field of wireless communications.

In this chapter, we will explore the various models used to describe and analyze wireless networks. These models are mathematical representations of the physical phenomena that govern the behavior of wireless signals. They are essential tools for engineers and researchers who design and study wireless networks.

We will begin by discussing the basic concepts of wireless networks, including the different types of wireless networks and the key components that make them up. We will then move on to more advanced topics, such as the propagation of wireless signals, the effects of interference, and the design of wireless network protocols.

Throughout the chapter, we will use mathematical expressions and equations to describe the concepts and models. Underwater acoustic communication is a form of communication that uses sound waves to transmit information underwater. It is particularly useful in deep-sea communication, where other forms of communication, such as radio waves, are not effective due to their high attenuation.

MIMO technology can be used to improve the reliability and data throughput of underwater acoustic communication. By using multiple antennas at both the transmitter and receiver, MIMO can exploit the multipath propagation to increase the data rate and improve the reliability of the communication link. This is particularly important in deep-sea communication, where the communication link is often subject to high levels of multipath propagation due to the presence of various obstacles such as water, sediment, and marine life.

In addition to underwater acoustic communication, MIMO technology can also be used in other forms of underwater communication, such as underwater optical communication and underwater radar. By exploiting the multipath propagation, MIMO can improve the reliability and data throughput of these communication systems, making them more effective for various applications, such as underwater exploration and surveillance.

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 2 of "Principles of Wireless Communications: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will delve into the fundamental concepts of modulation and coding, two crucial components of wireless communication systems.

Modulation is the process of converting information from one form to another, typically from digital to analog, for transmission over a communication channel. It is a critical step in wireless communication as it allows us to transmit information over long distances and through various mediums. We will explore the different types of modulation techniques, their advantages, and their applications.

Coding, on the other hand, is the process of adding redundancy to information to improve its reliability during transmission. In wireless communication, coding is used to combat the effects of noise and interference, which are inevitable in wireless channels. We will discuss the principles of coding, its types, and its role in wireless communication.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical expressions and equations to explain these concepts. For instance, we might represent a modulated signal as `$y_j(n)$` and an equation for coding as `$$
\Delta w = ...
$$`. These expressions will be rendered using the popular MathJax library.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of modulation and coding, their importance in wireless communication, and how they are used in practice. So, let's embark on this exciting journey of exploring the principles of wireless communications.




### Section: 2.1 Digital Modulation Techniques:

Digital modulation techniques are a crucial part of wireless communication systems. They are used to convert digital data into analog signals for transmission over a communication channel. In this section, we will explore the various digital modulation techniques, their principles, and their applications.

#### 2.1a Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Amplitude Modulation (AM) is a type of modulation where the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the amplitude of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of an AM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c[1 + m(t)]\cos(2\pi f_c t)
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $m(t)$ is the message signal, and $f_c$ is the carrier frequency.

AM has several advantages, including its simplicity and robustness against noise. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can disrupt the received signal.

#### 2.1b Frequency Modulation Techniques

Frequency Modulation (FM) is another type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the frequency of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of an FM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos[2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t)]
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta\phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

FM is less susceptible to frequency-selective fading compared to AM. However, it is more complex and requires more bandwidth for the same data rate compared to AM.

#### 2.1c Phase Modulation Techniques

Phase Modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the phase of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of a PM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

PM is less susceptible to frequency-selective fading compared to AM and FM. However, it is more complex and requires more bandwidth for the same data rate compared to AM.

#### 2.1d Quadrature Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) is a type of modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

QAM is a high-speed modulation technique that can achieve high data rates. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading and requires careful design to achieve reliable communication.

In the next section, we will delve into the principles of coding, another crucial component of wireless communication systems.

#### 2.1e Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing Techniques

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM) is a digital modulation technique that divides the available bandwidth into multiple subcarriers, each carrying a lower rate data stream. This technique is used in many modern wireless communication systems, including Wi-Fi and 4G/5G cellular networks.

The mathematical representation of an OFDM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{k=0}^{N-1} A_k\cos(2\pi f_k t)
$$

where $A_k$ is the amplitude of the $k$-th subcarrier, $f_k$ is the frequency of the $k$-th subcarrier, and $N$ is the total number of subcarriers.

OFDM offers several advantages over other modulation techniques. It is robust against frequency-selective fading, and it allows for the use of advanced coding techniques to achieve high data rates. However, it also requires a larger bandwidth compared to other modulation techniques for the same data rate.

#### 2.1f Single-Sideband Modulation Techniques

Single-Sideband Modulation (SSB) is a digital modulation technique that transmits only one of the two sidebands of the carrier signal, along with the carrier signal itself. This technique is used in many amateur radio applications to reduce power consumption and bandwidth requirements.

The mathematical representation of an SSB signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t) + A_s\cos(2\pi f_s t)
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, $A_s$ is the amplitude of the sideband signal, and $f_s$ is the sideband frequency.

SSB offers the advantage of reduced bandwidth requirements, but it also requires more complex receiver designs compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1g Multiple Frequency Shift Keying Techniques

Multiple Frequency Shift Keying (MFSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses multiple frequency shifts to represent digital data. This technique is used in many wireless communication systems, including Wi-Fi and Bluetooth devices.

The mathematical representation of an MFSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant, and $m(t)$ is the digital data stream.

MFSK offers the advantage of robustness against frequency-selective fading, but it also requires more complex receiver designs compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1h Multiple Phase Shift Keying Techniques

Multiple Phase Shift Keying (MPSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses multiple phase shifts to represent digital data. This technique is used in many wireless communication systems, including Wi-Fi and Bluetooth devices.

The mathematical representation of an MPSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant, and $m(t)$ is the digital data stream.

MPSK offers the advantage of robustness against frequency-selective fading, but it also requires more complex receiver designs compared to other modulation techniques.

The mathematical representation of an MQAM signal can be expressed as:

$$

### Section: 2.1 Digital Modulation Techniques:

Digital modulation techniques are a crucial part of wireless communication systems. They are used to convert digital data into analog signals for transmission over a communication channel. In this section, we will explore the various digital modulation techniques, their principles, and their applications.

#### 2.1a Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Amplitude Modulation (AM) is a type of modulation where the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the amplitude of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of an AM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c[1 + m(t)]\cos(2\pi f_c t)
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $m(t)$ is the message signal, and $f_c$ is the carrier frequency.

AM has several advantages, including its simplicity and robustness against noise. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can disrupt the received signal.

#### 2.1b Frequency Modulation Techniques

Frequency Modulation (FM) is another type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the frequency of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of an FM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta\phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

FM has the advantage of being less susceptible to frequency-selective fading compared to AM. However, it requires a larger bandwidth to transmit the same amount of information, which can be a disadvantage in systems with limited bandwidth.

#### 2.1c Phase Modulation Techniques

Phase Modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the phase of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of a PM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

PM has the advantage of being less susceptible to noise compared to AM and FM. However, it requires a larger bandwidth to transmit the same amount of information, which can be a disadvantage in systems with limited bandwidth.

#### 2.1d Quadrature Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) is a type of modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit multiple bits per symbol, which can increase the data rate of the communication system. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated receiver algorithms compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1e Single-Sideband Modulation Techniques

Single-Sideband Modulation (SSB) is a type of modulation where only one sideband of the carrier signal is transmitted, while the other sideband is suppressed. This reduces the bandwidth of the transmitted signal, which can be beneficial in systems with limited bandwidth.

The mathematical representation of an SSB signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) - A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

SSB has the advantage of being able to transmit the same amount of information with a smaller bandwidth compared to other modulation techniques. However, it requires more complex receiver algorithms compared to other modulation techniques.

### Subsection: 2.1b Frequency Modulation Techniques

Frequency Modulation (FM) is a type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the frequency of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of an FM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta\phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

FM has the advantage of being less susceptible to frequency-selective fading compared to AM. However, it requires a larger bandwidth to transmit the same amount of information, which can be a disadvantage in systems with limited bandwidth.

#### 2.1b.1 Single-Sideband Modulation

Single-Sideband Modulation (SSB) is a type of FM where only one sideband of the carrier signal is transmitted, while the other sideband is suppressed. This reduces the bandwidth of the transmitted signal, which can be beneficial in systems with limited bandwidth.

The mathematical representation of an SSB signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t)) - A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta\phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

SSB has the advantage of being able to transmit the same amount of information with a smaller bandwidth compared to other modulation techniques. However, it requires more complex receiver algorithms compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1b.2 Quadrature Amplitude Modulation

Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) is a type of FM where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta\phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit multiple bits per symbol, which can increase the data rate of the communication system. However, it requires more complex receiver algorithms compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1b.3 Single-Sideband Modulation with Quadrature Amplitude Modulation

Single-Sideband Modulation with Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (SSB-QAM) is a type of FM where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied, and only one sideband of the carrier signal is transmitted. This modulation technique combines the advantages of SSB and QAM, making it a popular choice in wireless communication systems.

The mathematical representation of a SSB-QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t)) - A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t)) - A_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + \Delta\phi(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta\phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

SSB-QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit multiple bits per symbol with a smaller bandwidth compared to other modulation techniques. However, it requires more complex receiver algorithms compared to other modulation techniques.





#### 2.1c Phase Modulation Techniques

Phase Modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The carrier signal is a high-frequency sinusoidal wave, and the digital data is represented by the phase of the carrier signal.

The mathematical representation of a PM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

PM has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to phase noise, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1d Quadrature Amplitude Modulation

Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) is a type of modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied to represent digital data. The carrier signal is composed of two orthogonal components, one for the in-phase (I) component and one for the quadrature (Q) component. The digital data is represented by the combination of the I and Q components.

The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) + jA_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to phase noise and amplitude distortion, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1e Minimum Shift Keying

Minimum Shift Keying (MSK) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in a minimum manner to represent digital data. The carrier signal is composed of two orthogonal components, one for the in-phase (I) component and one for the quadrature (Q) component. The digital data is represented by the combination of the I and Q components.

The mathematical representation of an MSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) + jA_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

MSK has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to phase noise and amplitude distortion, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1f Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM) is a type of modulation where multiple subcarriers are used to transmit digital data. Each subcarrier can be modulated using any of the modulation techniques discussed above, such as PM, QAM, or MSK. The digital data is represented by the combination of the subcarriers.

The mathematical representation of an OFDM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on subcarrier $i$, and $N$ is the number of subcarriers.

OFDM has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1g Spread Spectrum Techniques

Spread Spectrum Techniques are a type of modulation where the signal is spread over a wide frequency band. This is achieved by multiplying the signal with a pseudo-random sequence, known as a spreading code. The receiver must know the same spreading code to de-spread the received signal and recover the original data.

The mathematical representation of a spread spectrum signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on subcarrier $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on subcarrier $i$, and $N$ is the number of subcarriers.

Spread spectrum techniques have the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making them suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, they are susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1h Frequency Hopping Spread Spectrum

Frequency Hopping Spread Spectrum (FHSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal hops between different frequencies within a given bandwidth. This technique is used to combat interference and improve the reliability of the transmitted signal.

The mathematical representation of an FHSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on frequency $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on frequency $i$, and $N$ is the number of frequencies.

FHSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1i Direct Sequence Spread Spectrum

Direct Sequence Spread Spectrum (DSSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal is spread over a wide frequency band by multiplying it with a pseudo-random sequence, known as a spreading code. The receiver must know the same spreading code to de-spread the received signal and recover the original data.

The mathematical representation of a DSSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on subcarrier $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on subcarrier $i$, and $N$ is the number of subcarriers.

DSSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1j Time Hopping Spread Spectrum

Time Hopping Spread Spectrum (THSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal is spread over a wide time domain by hopping between different time slots. This technique is used to combat interference and improve the reliability of the transmitted signal.

The mathematical representation of a THSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on time slot $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on time slot $i$, and $N$ is the number of time slots.

THSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to time-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1k Chirp Spread Spectrum

Chirp Spread Spectrum (CSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal is spread over a wide frequency band by varying the carrier frequency of the signal. This technique is used to combat interference and improve the reliability of the transmitted signal.

$$

#### 2.1Â² Chirp Spread Spectrum

Chirp Spread Spectrum (CSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal is spread over a wide frequency band by varying the carrier frequency of the signal. This technique is used to combat interference and improve the reliability of the transmitted signal.

The mathematical representation of a CSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on frequency $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on frequency $i$, and $N$ is the number of frequencies.

CSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1Â³ Frequency Hopping Spread Spectrum

Frequency Hopping Spread Spectrum (FHSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal hops between different frequencies within a given bandwidth. This technique is used to combat interference and improve the reliability of the transmitted signal.

The mathematical representation of an FHSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on frequency $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on frequency $i$, and $N$ is the number of frequencies.

FHSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1â´ Direct Sequence Spread Spectrum

Direct Sequence Spread Spectrum (DSSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal is spread over a wide frequency band by multiplying it with a pseudo-random sequence, known as a spreading code. The receiver must know the same spreading code to de-spread the received signal and recover the original data.

The mathematical representation of a DSSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on frequency $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on frequency $i$, and $N$ is the number of frequencies.

DSSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to frequency-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1âµ Time Hopping Spread Spectrum

Time Hopping Spread Spectrum (THSS) is a type of spread spectrum technique where the signal is spread over a wide time domain by hopping between different time slots. This technique is used to combat interference and improve the reliability of the transmitted signal.

The mathematical representation of a THSS signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{N}s_i(t)c_i(t)
$$

where $s_i(t)$ is the signal on time slot $i$, $c_i(t)$ is the spreading code on time slot $i$, and $N$ is the number of time slots.

THSS has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to time-selective fading, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

#### 2.1â¶ Chirp Sp


#### 2.1d Quadrature Modulation Techniques

Quadrature modulation techniques are a class of modulation schemes that involve the use of two orthogonal carriers, one for the in-phase (I) component and one for the quadrature (Q) component. These techniques are used in a variety of applications, including wireless communications, satellite communications, and optical communications.

##### Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM)

As previously discussed in section 2.1c, QAM is a type of quadrature modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied to represent digital data. The carrier signal is composed of two orthogonal components, one for the in-phase (I) component and one for the quadrature (Q) component. The digital data is represented by the combination of the I and Q components.

The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) + jA_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit a large amount of information in a given bandwidth, making it suitable for high-speed data transmission. However, it is susceptible to phase noise and amplitude distortion, which can degrade the quality of the received signal.

##### Quadrature Phase Shift Keying (QPSK)

Quadrature Phase Shift Keying (QPSK) is another type of quadrature modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied to represent digital data. Unlike QAM, the amplitude of the carrier signal remains constant in QPSK.

The mathematical representation of a QPSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

QPSK has the advantage of being less susceptible to phase noise and amplitude distortion compared to QAM. However, it can only transmit half the amount of information in a given bandwidth as QAM.

##### Quadrature Differential Phase Shift Keying (QDPSK)

Quadrature Differential Phase Shift Keying (QDPSK) is a variation of QPSK where the phase difference between consecutive symbols is used to represent digital data. This technique is particularly useful in applications where the phase of the carrier signal is not well-defined, such as in optical communications.

The mathematical representation of a QDPSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_p(m(t) - m(t - T)))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant, and $T$ is the symbol period.

QDPSK has the advantage of being less susceptible to phase noise and amplitude distortion compared to QAM and QPSK. However, it can only transmit a quarter of the amount of information in a given bandwidth as QAM.

##### Quadrature Offset Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QOQAM)

Quadrature Offset Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QOQAM) is a variation of QAM where the I and Q components are offset in frequency. This technique is used to improve the spectral efficiency of QAM, particularly in applications where the bandwidth is limited.

The mathematical representation of a QOQAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t)) + jA_c\sin(2\pi f_c t + k_pm(t))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant. The offset in frequency is typically achieved by using two different carrier frequencies for the I and Q components.

QOQAM has the advantage of being able to transmit a larger amount of information in a given bandwidth compared to QAM. However, it is more susceptible to phase noise and amplitude distortion.

#### 2.1e Minimum Shift Keying

Minimum Shift Keying (MSK) is a type of digital modulation technique that is used in wireless communications. It is a form of quadrature modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied to represent digital data. MSK is particularly useful in applications where the bandwidth is limited and the signal needs to be transmitted with minimum distortion.

##### Mathematical Formulation of MSK

The mathematical representation of an MSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_p(m(t) - m(t - T)))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant, and $T$ is the symbol period. The term $m(t)$ represents the digital data being transmitted, and $m(t - T)$ represents the digital data transmitted in the previous symbol period.

##### Advantages of MSK

MSK has several advantages over other modulation techniques. One of the key advantages is its ability to provide a constant envelope, which makes it less susceptible to non-linear distortion. This is particularly useful in wireless communications where the signal may pass through non-linear devices.

Another advantage of MSK is its ability to provide a constant bandwidth, which makes it suitable for applications where the bandwidth is limited. This is achieved by varying the phase of the carrier signal, rather than its amplitude, which allows for a constant bandwidth.

##### Disadvantages of MSK

Despite its advantages, MSK also has some disadvantages. One of the main disadvantages is its lower spectral efficiency compared to other modulation techniques such as QAM and QPSK. This is because MSK can only transmit two bits per symbol, while QAM and QPSK can transmit four and two bits per symbol respectively.

Another disadvantage of MSK is its susceptibility to phase noise. Since the phase of the carrier signal is varied to represent digital data, any phase noise can significantly degrade the quality of the received signal.

##### Applications of MSK

MSK is widely used in wireless communications, particularly in satellite communications and wireless local area networks (WLANs). It is also used in optical communications, where the bandwidth is limited and the signal needs to be transmitted with minimum distortion.

In the next section, we will discuss another type of digital modulation technique, known as Quadrature Phase Shift Keying (QPSK).

#### 2.1f Frequency Shift Keying

Frequency Shift Keying (FSK) is another type of digital modulation technique that is used in wireless communications. It is a form of analog modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied to represent digital data. FSK is particularly useful in applications where the bandwidth is limited and the signal needs to be transmitted with minimum distortion.

##### Mathematical Formulation of FSK

The mathematical representation of an FSK signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_c t + k_p(m(t) - m(t - T)))
$$

where $A_c$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant, and $T$ is the symbol period. The term $m(t)$ represents the digital data being transmitted, and $m(t - T)$ represents the digital data transmitted in the previous symbol period.

##### Advantages of FSK

FSK has several advantages over other modulation techniques. One of the key advantages is its ability to provide a constant envelope, which makes it less susceptible to non-linear distortion. This is particularly useful in wireless communications where the signal may pass through non-linear devices.

Another advantage of FSK is its ability to provide a constant bandwidth, which makes it suitable for applications where the bandwidth is limited. This is achieved by varying the frequency of the carrier signal, rather than its amplitude, which allows for a constant bandwidth.

##### Disadvantages of FSK

Despite its advantages, FSK also has some disadvantages. One of the main disadvantages is its lower spectral efficiency compared to other modulation techniques such as MSK and QAM. This is because FSK can only transmit two bits per symbol, while MSK and QAM can transmit four and four bits per symbol respectively.

Another disadvantage of FSK is its susceptibility to frequency noise. Since the frequency of the carrier signal is varied to represent digital data, any frequency noise can significantly degrade the quality of the received signal.

##### Applications of FSK

FSK is widely used in wireless communications, particularly in applications where the bandwidth is limited and the signal needs to be transmitted with minimum distortion. It is also used in applications where the signal needs to be transmitted with a constant envelope, such as in satellite communications.




#### 2.2a Turbo Codes

Turbo codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1990s. They were designed to provide near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. Turbo codes are used in a variety of applications, including wireless communications, satellite communications, and optical communications.

##### Introduction to Turbo Codes

Turbo codes are a type of linear block code that uses two parallel convolutional encoders and two parallel convolutional decoders. The encoders and decoders are connected in a way that allows for the exchange of information between them. This exchange of information is what gives turbo codes their name - they are like two codes working together, or "turbocharging" each other.

The mathematical representation of a turbo code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{G} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{G}_1 & \mathbf{0} \\
\mathbf{0} & \mathbf{G}_2
\end{bmatrix}, \quad
\mathbf{H} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{H}_1 & \mathbf{H}_2
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\mathbf{G}_1$ and $\mathbf{G}_2$ are the generator matrices of the two parallel convolutional encoders, and $\mathbf{H}_1$ and $\mathbf{H}_2$ are the parity-check matrices of the two parallel convolutional decoders.

Turbo codes have the advantage of being able to achieve near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. However, they also have the disadvantage of being more complex to implement compared to other types of error control codes.

##### Applications of Turbo Codes

Turbo codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities. They are used in satellite communications, wireless communications, and optical communications. They are also used in deep space communications, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will discuss another type of error control code, the low-density parity-check (LDPC) code.

#### 2.2b Low-Density Parity-Check Codes

Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) codes are another type of error control code that have gained popularity in recent years. They were first introduced in the 1960s, but it wasn't until the 1990s that their potential was fully realized. LDPC codes are used in a variety of applications, including wireless communications, satellite communications, and optical communications.

##### Introduction to Low-Density Parity-Check Codes

LDPC codes are a type of linear block code that have a low density of parity-checks. This means that the parity-check matrix $\mathbf{H}$ has a large number of zero entries. The density of the parity-check matrix is defined as the ratio of the number of non-zero entries to the total number of entries in the matrix. For LDPC codes, this density is typically very low, often less than 0.1.

The mathematical representation of an LDPC code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{G} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{G}_1 & \mathbf{0} \\
\mathbf{0} & \mathbf{G}_2
\end{bmatrix}, \quad
\mathbf{H} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{H}_1 & \mathbf{H}_2
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\mathbf{G}_1$ and $\mathbf{G}_2$ are the generator matrices of the two parallel convolutional encoders, and $\mathbf{H}_1$ and $\mathbf{H}_2$ are the parity-check matrices of the two parallel convolutional decoders.

LDPC codes have the advantage of being able to achieve near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. However, they also have the disadvantage of being more complex to implement compared to other types of error control codes.

##### Applications of Low-Density Parity-Check Codes

LDPC codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities. They are used in satellite communications, wireless communications, and optical communications. They are also used in deep space communications, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will discuss another type of error control code, the turbo codes.

#### 2.2c Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are another type of error control code that have been widely used in communication systems. They were first introduced in the 1960s and have since been extensively studied and applied in various communication scenarios. Convolutional codes are used in a variety of applications, including wireless communications, satellite communications, and optical communications.

##### Introduction to Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a type of linear block code that are particularly well-suited for applications where the channel conditions can change rapidly over time. They are a type of continuous-memory code, meaning that the code depends on the entire history of the input sequence, not just the current symbol. This makes them particularly effective in environments where the channel conditions can change rapidly, as they can adapt to these changes.

The mathematical representation of a convolutional code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{G} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{G}_1 & \mathbf{0} \\
\mathbf{0} & \mathbf{G}_2
\end{bmatrix}, \quad
\mathbf{H} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{H}_1 & \mathbf{H}_2
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\mathbf{G}_1$ and $\mathbf{G}_2$ are the generator matrices of the two parallel convolutional encoders, and $\mathbf{H}_1$ and $\mathbf{H}_2$ are the parity-check matrices of the two parallel convolutional decoders.

Convolutional codes have the advantage of being able to achieve near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. However, they also have the disadvantage of being more complex to implement compared to other types of error control codes.

##### Applications of Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities. They are used in satellite communications, wireless communications, and optical communications. They are also used in deep space communications, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will discuss another type of error control code, the low-density parity-check (LDPC) codes.

#### 2.2d Polar Codes

Polar codes are a relatively new type of error control code that have gained significant attention in recent years. They were first introduced in 2009 and have since been extensively studied and applied in various communication scenarios. Polar codes are used in a variety of applications, including wireless communications, satellite communications, and optical communications.

##### Introduction to Polar Codes

Polar codes are a type of linear block code that are particularly well-suited for applications where the channel conditions can change rapidly over time. They are a type of discrete-memory code, meaning that the code depends on the current symbol and a finite number of previous symbols. This makes them particularly effective in environments where the channel conditions can change rapidly, as they can adapt to these changes.

The mathematical representation of a polar code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{G} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{G}_1 & \mathbf{0} \\
\mathbf{0} & \mathbf{G}_2
\end{bmatrix}, \quad
\mathbf{H} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{H}_1 & \mathbf{H}_2
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\mathbf{G}_1$ and $\mathbf{G}_2$ are the generator matrices of the two parallel convolutional encoders, and $\mathbf{H}_1$ and $\mathbf{H}_2$ are the parity-check matrices of the two parallel convolutional decoders.

Polar codes have the advantage of being able to achieve near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. However, they also have the disadvantage of being more complex to implement compared to other types of error control codes.

##### Applications of Polar Codes

Polar codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities. They are used in satellite communications, wireless communications, and optical communications. They are also used in deep space communications, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will discuss another type of error control code, the low-density parity-check (LDPC) codes.

## Chapter: Multiple Access Techniques

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3: Multiple Access Techniques. This chapter is dedicated to exploring the principles of multiple access techniques, a crucial aspect of wireless communications. Multiple access techniques are the methods used to allow multiple users to share the same communication medium, such as a wireless channel. This is essential in wireless communication systems, as they often need to serve multiple users simultaneously.

In this chapter, we will delve into the various types of multiple access techniques, including Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA), Frequency Division Multiple Access (FDMA), and Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA). Each of these techniques has its own unique characteristics and applications, and understanding them is key to designing efficient and reliable wireless communication systems.

We will also discuss the challenges and solutions associated with implementing these techniques in real-world scenarios. This includes dealing with issues such as interference, power control, and synchronization. We will also explore how these techniques are used in different types of wireless communication systems, from cellular networks to satellite communications.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles of multiple access techniques and be able to apply this knowledge to the design and analysis of wireless communication systems. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field, this chapter will provide you with the tools and knowledge you need to navigate the complex world of multiple access techniques.

So, let's embark on this journey of exploring the principles of multiple access techniques in wireless communications.




#### 2.2b LDPC Codes

Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1960s. They are a type of linear block code that uses a sparse parity-check matrix to achieve good error correction capabilities. LDPC codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities and their ability to achieve near-Shannon limit performance.

##### Introduction to LDPC Codes

LDPC codes are a type of linear block code that uses a sparse parity-check matrix to encode and decode information. The parity-check matrix, denoted as $\mathbf{H}$, is sparse, meaning it has a large number of zero entries. This sparsity allows for efficient encoding and decoding, making LDPC codes attractive for practical applications.

The mathematical representation of an LDPC code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{H} = \begin{bmatrix}
\mathbf{H}_1 & \mathbf{H}_2 & \cdots & \mathbf{H}_n
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\mathbf{H}_i$ is the parity-check matrix for the $i$-th row of the code. Each $\mathbf{H}_i$ is a binary matrix of size $k \times n$, where $k$ is the number of parity-check equations and $n$ is the code length.

LDPC codes have the advantage of being able to achieve near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. However, they also have the disadvantage of being more complex to implement compared to other types of error control codes.

##### Applications of LDPC Codes

LDPC codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities. They are used in satellite communications, wireless communications, and optical communications. They are also used in deep space communications, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties and performance of LDPC codes, and discuss some of the techniques used to implement them efficiently.

#### 2.2c Turbo and LDPC Codes

Turbo and LDPC codes are two of the most powerful error control codes used in wireless communications. Both codes have been designed to achieve near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. In this section, we will compare and contrast these two codes, discussing their properties, performance, and applications.

##### Introduction to Turbo and LDPC Codes

Turbo codes are a type of linear block code that uses two parallel convolutional encoders and two parallel convolutional decoders. The encoders and decoders are connected in a way that allows for the exchange of information between them. This exchange of information is what gives turbo codes their name - they are like two codes working together, or "turbocharging" each other.

LDPC codes, on the other hand, are a type of linear block code that uses a sparse parity-check matrix to encode and decode information. The parity-check matrix, denoted as $\mathbf{H}$, is sparse, meaning it has a large number of zero entries. This sparsity allows for efficient encoding and decoding, making LDPC codes attractive for practical applications.

##### Comparison of Turbo and LDPC Codes

Both turbo and LDPC codes have excellent error correction capabilities. However, they differ in their implementation and performance. Turbo codes are more complex to implement compared to LDPC codes, but they have the advantage of being able to achieve near-Shannon limit performance with a relatively small code length. LDPC codes, on the other hand, are easier to implement but require a longer code length to achieve near-Shannon limit performance.

In terms of performance, turbo codes have been shown to have better error correction capabilities than LDPC codes for short code lengths. However, as the code length increases, the performance of LDPC codes improves and can surpass that of turbo codes.

##### Applications of Turbo and LDPC Codes

Both turbo and LDPC codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems due to their excellent error correction capabilities. They are used in satellite communications, wireless communications, and optical communications. They are also used in deep space communications, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties and performance of these codes, discussing their decoding algorithms and their applications in more detail.




#### 2.2c Channel Coding Theorems

Channel coding theorems are fundamental results in information theory that provide upper bounds on the error probability of a code. These theorems are crucial in the design and analysis of error control codes, including LDPC codes. In this section, we will introduce some of the key channel coding theorems and discuss their implications for LDPC codes.

##### Introduction to Channel Coding Theorems

Channel coding theorems are mathematical results that provide upper bounds on the error probability of a code. These theorems are crucial in the design and analysis of error control codes, as they provide a theoretical limit on the performance of a code. The most well-known channel coding theorems include the Hamming Bound, the Singleton Bound, and the Gilbert-Varshamov Bound.

The Hamming Bound, named after the American mathematician Richard Hamming, provides an upper bound on the error probability of a code. It states that the error probability of a code is at most equal to the probability of a single-bit error. This bound is particularly useful for binary codes, where the codewords are binary vectors.

The Singleton Bound, named after the British mathematician Peter Singleton, provides an upper bound on the error probability of a code. It states that the error probability of a code is at most equal to the probability of a single-bit error, plus the probability of a double-bit error. This bound is particularly useful for binary codes, where the codewords are binary vectors.

The Gilbert-Varshamov Bound, named after the American mathematician Irving S. Gilbert and the Russian mathematician Boris Varshamov, provides an upper bound on the error probability of a code. It states that the error probability of a code is at most equal to the probability of a single-bit error, plus the probability of a double-bit error, plus the probability of a triple-bit error, and so on. This bound is particularly useful for binary codes, where the codewords are binary vectors.

##### Applications of Channel Coding Theorems

Channel coding theorems have many applications in the design and analysis of error control codes. They provide a theoretical limit on the performance of a code, which can be used to guide the design of a code. For example, the Hamming Bound can be used to determine the minimum distance of a code, which is a key parameter in the design of a code.

In the context of LDPC codes, channel coding theorems can be used to analyze the performance of these codes. For example, the Singleton Bound can be used to determine the maximum number of parity-check equations that can be used in an LDPC code, without violating the bound. This can be useful in the design of LDPC codes, as it provides a guide for the number of parity-check equations that can be used in the code.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties and performance of LDPC codes, and discuss some of the techniques used to design these codes.




#### 2.2d Error Correction Techniques

Error correction techniques are essential in wireless communications, as they allow for the detection and correction of errors that occur during the transmission of information. These techniques are crucial in ensuring the reliability and integrity of transmitted data. In this section, we will introduce some of the key error correction techniques and discuss their applications in wireless communications.

##### Introduction to Error Correction Techniques

Error correction techniques are methods used to detect and correct errors that occur during the transmission of information. These techniques are crucial in wireless communications, as they allow for the reliable transmission of data even in the presence of noise and interference. The most well-known error correction techniques include parity check codes, cyclic redundancy check (CRC), and forward error correction (FEC).

Parity check codes are the simplest form of error correction codes. They work by adding an extra bit to the transmitted data, known as a parity bit. The parity bit is calculated based on the number of 1s in the data, and it is used to detect an odd number of 1s, which indicates an error. However, parity check codes can only detect an odd number of errors, and they cannot correct any errors.

Cyclic redundancy check (CRC) is a more advanced error correction technique that is commonly used in data communication protocols. It works by adding a fixed number of redundant bits to the transmitted data, known as a CRC code. The CRC code is calculated based on the data and is used to detect and correct single-bit errors. CRC is particularly useful in situations where the data is transmitted in blocks, as it can check the entire block of data at once.

Forward error correction (FEC) is a more complex error correction technique that is used in situations where the data is transmitted in a continuous stream. It works by adding redundant bits to the transmitted data, known as FEC codes. These codes are calculated based on the data and are used to detect and correct multiple-bit errors. FEC is particularly useful in situations where the data is transmitted over a noisy channel, as it can provide a high level of error correction.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the principles of error control coding and discuss some of the key techniques used in wireless communications.




#### 2.3a Shannon Capacity

The Shannon Capacity, named after the American mathematician Claude Shannon, is a fundamental concept in information theory that measures the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel. It is a key concept in wireless communications, as it provides a theoretical limit on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel.

The Shannon Capacity is defined as the maximum mutual information between the input and output of a channel, given the channel's noise characteristics. In other words, it is the maximum amount of information that can be transmitted over the channel without error, given the channel's noise characteristics.

The Shannon Capacity is particularly important in wireless communications, as it provides a theoretical limit on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel. This limit is crucial in the design of wireless communication systems, as it helps engineers understand the fundamental limits of what is possible and guide the design of practical systems.

The Shannon Capacity is also closely related to the concept of channel capacity, which is the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error. The Shannon Capacity is essentially the channel capacity of a noisy channel.

The Shannon Capacity is defined as follows:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $C$ is the Shannon Capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$.

The Shannon Capacity is a key concept in information theory and wireless communications. It provides a theoretical limit on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel, and it is crucial in the design of wireless communication systems. In the next section, we will discuss the concept of channel capacity in more detail and explore its implications for wireless communications.

#### 2.3b Channel Capacity

The concept of channel capacity is closely related to the Shannon Capacity. As mentioned earlier, the Shannon Capacity is essentially the channel capacity of a noisy channel. The channel capacity, denoted as $C$, is defined as the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error. In other words, it is the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a channel, given the channel's noise characteristics.

The channel capacity is a fundamental concept in wireless communications, as it provides a theoretical limit on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error. This limit is crucial in the design of wireless communication systems, as it helps engineers understand the fundamental limits of what is possible and guide the design of practical systems.

The channel capacity is defined as follows:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$.

The mutual information $I(X;Y)$ is a measure of the amount of information that is shared between two random variables $X$ and $Y$. It is defined as the difference between the entropy of $Y$ and the conditional entropy of $Y$ given $X$:

$$
I(X;Y) = H(Y) - H(Y|X)
$$

where $H(Y)$ is the entropy of $Y$, and $H(Y|X)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y$ given $X$.

The channel capacity $C$ is essentially the maximum mutual information between the input and output of a channel, given the channel's noise characteristics. This means that the channel capacity represents the maximum amount of information that can be transmitted over the channel without error, given the channel's noise characteristics.

The concept of channel capacity is particularly important in wireless communications, as it provides a theoretical limit on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel. This limit is crucial in the design of wireless communication systems, as it helps engineers understand the fundamental limits of what is possible and guide the design of practical systems.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of channel capacity in more detail and explore its implications for wireless communications.

#### 2.3c Channel Capacity and Modulation

The concept of channel capacity is closely tied to the process of modulation. Modulation is a technique used in wireless communications to convert digital data into analog signals for transmission over a channel. The channel capacity plays a crucial role in determining the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error, and modulation techniques are designed to approach this limit.

The channel capacity $C$ is essentially the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error. This means that any modulation scheme that achieves a rate higher than the channel capacity will inevitably introduce errors in the transmitted data. Therefore, the goal of modulation is to design schemes that approach the channel capacity while minimizing errors.

The process of modulation can be represented mathematically as follows:

$$
s(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{M} a_i(t)g_i(t)
$$

where $s(t)$ is the modulated signal, $a_i(t)$ is the $i$-th modulating signal, and $g_i(t)$ is the $i$-th basis function. The modulating signals $a_i(t)$ are typically binary sequences, and the basis functions $g_i(t)$ are orthogonal to each other.

The modulation process can be visualized as a mapping from the input data (represented by the modulating signals $a_i(t)$) to the modulated signal $s(t)$. The goal of modulation is to design this mapping in such a way that the modulated signal $s(t)$ is as close to the channel capacity $C$ as possible, while minimizing errors.

In the next section, we will discuss some common modulation techniques and how they relate to the concept of channel capacity.

#### 2.3d Channel Capacity and Coding

The concept of channel capacity is also closely tied to the process of coding. Coding is a technique used in wireless communications to add redundancy to the transmitted data, which helps to detect and correct errors. The channel capacity plays a crucial role in determining the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error, and coding techniques are designed to approach this limit.

The channel capacity $C$ is essentially the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a channel without error. This means that any coding scheme that achieves a rate higher than the channel capacity will inevitably introduce errors in the transmitted data. Therefore, the goal of coding is to design schemes that approach the channel capacity while minimizing errors.

The process of coding can be represented mathematically as follows:

$$
c(x) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} b_i(x)h_i(x)
$$

where $c(x)$ is the coded data, $b_i(x)$ is the $i$-th coding vector, and $h_i(x)$ is the $i$-th basis function. The coding vectors $b_i(x)$ are typically binary sequences, and the basis functions $h_i(x)$ are orthogonal to each other.

The coding process can be visualized as a mapping from the input data (represented by the coding vectors $b_i(x)$) to the coded data $c(x)$. The goal of coding is to design this mapping in such a way that the coded data $c(x)$ is as close to the channel capacity $C$ as possible, while minimizing errors.

In the next section, we will discuss some common coding techniques and how they relate to the concept of channel capacity.
