## Chapter 3: Wireless Networks:




### Introduction

Wireless communication has become an integral part of our daily lives, enabling us to stay connected and access information from anywhere. With the increasing demand for wireless communication, the need for efficient and reliable multiple access techniques has become crucial. In this chapter, we will delve into the principles of multiple access techniques, which are essential for managing the limited wireless spectrum and enabling multiple users to share the same wireless medium.

Multiple access techniques are used to allow multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. This is achieved by dividing the channel into smaller subchannels, each assigned to a specific user. The users then transmit and receive data on their assigned subchannels, allowing for efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum.

There are two main types of multiple access techniques: frequency division multiple access (FDMA) and time division multiple access (TDMA). FDMA divides the channel into multiple frequency bands, with each band assigned to a specific user. TDMA, on the other hand, divides the channel into multiple time slots, with each slot assigned to a specific user.

In addition to FDMA and TDMA, there are also other multiple access techniques such as code division multiple access (CDMA) and space division multiple access (SDMA). CDMA uses unique codes to differentiate between different users, allowing them to transmit and receive data simultaneously on the same frequency band. SDMA, on the other hand, uses different antennas to transmit and receive data, allowing for multiple users to access the same channel simultaneously.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles behind these multiple access techniques and their applications in wireless communication. We will also discuss the advantages and disadvantages of each technique, as well as their performance in different scenarios. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of multiple access techniques and their role in wireless communication.




### Subsection: 3.1a Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access (OFDMA) is a multiple access technique that is used in wireless communication systems. It is a form of frequency division multiple access (FDMA) where multiple users can access the same frequency band simultaneously by using different subcarriers. OFDMA is widely used in modern wireless communication systems, including Wi-Fi, LTE, and satellite communication.

#### Orthogonality in OFDMA

The key feature of OFDMA is the concept of orthogonality. In OFDMA, the subcarrier frequencies are chosen so that the subcarriers are orthogonal to each other. This means that there is no interference between the subchannels, and the subchannels can be transmitted simultaneously without interfering with each other. This is achieved by carefully selecting the subcarrier frequencies and ensuring that they are orthogonal to each other.

The orthogonality of subcarriers in OFDMA is achieved by using a discrete Fourier transform (DFT) to convert the digital data stream into multiple subcarriers. The DFT is a mathematical operation that decomposes a signal into its frequency components. In OFDMA, the DFT is used to divide the digital data stream into multiple subcarriers, each with a different frequency. These subcarriers are then transmitted simultaneously, and the receiver uses an inverse DFT to recover the original digital data stream.

#### Advantages and Disadvantages of OFDMA

The use of orthogonality in OFDMA has several advantages. One of the main advantages is the ability to transmit multiple users simultaneously on the same frequency band. This allows for more efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum and increases the capacity of the wireless communication system. Additionally, the orthogonality of subcarriers also reduces the interference between users, improving the overall quality of the communication.

However, OFDMA also has some disadvantages. One of the main disadvantages is the complexity of the receiver design. The receiver must be able to demodulate and decode multiple subcarriers simultaneously, which requires a more complex design compared to other multiple access techniques. Additionally, the use of orthogonality also requires strict frequency synchronization between the transmitter and receiver, which can be challenging to achieve in practice.

#### Conclusion

In conclusion, OFDMA is a powerful multiple access technique that is widely used in modern wireless communication systems. Its ability to transmit multiple users simultaneously on the same frequency band makes it a popular choice for efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum. However, its complexity and strict frequency synchronization requirements must also be considered when implementing OFDMA in a wireless communication system. 





### Subsection: 3.1b Space Division Multiple Access

Space Division Multiple Access (SDMA) is another multiple access technique that is used in wireless communication systems. Unlike OFDMA, which divides the frequency band into multiple subcarriers, SDMA divides the space (or physical location) into multiple cells, with each cell assigned to a different user. This allows for multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously without interfering with each other.

#### Principles of SDMA

The key principle of SDMA is the use of directional antennas. In SDMA, each user is assigned a unique directional antenna pattern, which is used to transmit and receive signals. This allows for the creation of multiple cells, each with its own unique antenna pattern. By using directional antennas, the signals from different users can be separated and received by their respective antennas, reducing interference and improving the overall quality of the communication.

The directional antenna patterns are typically created using advanced signal processing techniques, such as beamforming and spatial filtering. These techniques allow for the creation of multiple cells with different antenna patterns, each assigned to a different user. This allows for the efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum and increases the capacity of the wireless communication system.

#### Advantages and Disadvantages of SDMA

The use of directional antennas in SDMA has several advantages. One of the main advantages is the ability to transmit multiple users simultaneously on the same frequency band without interference. This allows for more efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum and increases the capacity of the wireless communication system. Additionally, SDMA also offers improved security, as the directional antennas can be used to limit the range of communication to a specific area, making it more difficult for unauthorized users to access the network.

However, SDMA also has some disadvantages. One of the main disadvantages is the complexity of the signal processing techniques required to create and maintain the directional antenna patterns. This can be a challenge for some wireless communication systems, especially those with limited resources. Additionally, SDMA may not be suitable for all types of wireless communication, such as in scenarios where users are constantly moving and changing their location.

In conclusion, SDMA is a powerful multiple access technique that offers many advantages for wireless communication systems. By using directional antennas and advanced signal processing techniques, SDMA allows for the efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum and increases the capacity of the system. However, it is important to consider the disadvantages and limitations of SDMA when deciding if it is the right multiple access technique for a particular wireless communication system.





### Subsection: 3.1c Code Division Multiple Access

Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA) is a multiple access technique that is used in wireless communication systems. Unlike FDMA and SDMA, which divide the frequency band or space into multiple channels, CDMA allows for multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously by using unique codes to differentiate between users.

#### Principles of CDMA

The key principle of CDMA is the use of unique codes for each user. In CDMA, each user is assigned a unique code, which is used to transmit and receive signals. This allows for multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously without interfering with each other. The unique codes are typically generated using advanced signal processing techniques, such as spreading and despreading.

The spreading and despreading process involves multiplying the transmitted signal by the assigned code, which allows for the receiver to distinguish between different users. This process also helps to reduce interference from other users, as the unique codes ensure that only the intended user can decode the transmitted signal.

#### Advantages and Disadvantages of CDMA

The use of unique codes in CDMA has several advantages. One of the main advantages is the ability to transmit multiple users simultaneously on the same frequency band without interference. This allows for more efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum and increases the capacity of the wireless communication system. Additionally, CDMA also offers improved security, as the unique codes can be used to limit the range of communication to a specific area, making it more difficult for unauthorized users to access the network.

However, CDMA also has some disadvantages. One of the main disadvantages is the susceptibility to interference from other users. As multiple users are accessing the same frequency band, interference can occur if the codes are not properly designed or if there are too many users in the system. This can lead to a decrease in the quality of the received signal and can cause errors in data transmission.

Another disadvantage of CDMA is the complexity of the receiver design. In order to decode the transmitted signals, the receiver must be able to distinguish between the different users and their assigned codes. This requires advanced signal processing techniques and can be costly to implement.

Despite these disadvantages, CDMA remains a popular multiple access technique in wireless communication systems due to its ability to efficiently utilize the limited wireless spectrum and its improved security features. With advancements in signal processing techniques and technology, the disadvantages of CDMA can be mitigated, making it a valuable tool in modern wireless communication systems.





### Subsection: 3.1d Time Division Multiple Access

Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA) is another multiple access technique used in wireless communication systems. Unlike FDMA and CDMA, which divide the frequency band or use unique codes, TDMA divides the time into multiple time slots, allowing multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously.

#### Principles of TDMA

The key principle of TDMA is the division of time into multiple time slots. Each user is assigned a specific time slot, and only during that time slot can they transmit or receive signals. This allows for multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously without interfering with each other. The time slots are typically assigned using advanced signal processing techniques, such as time division multiplexing and demultiplexing.

The time division multiplexing and demultiplexing process involves dividing the transmitted signal into multiple time slots, each assigned to a different user. This allows for the receiver to distinguish between different users and only decode the intended signal. This process also helps to reduce interference from other users, as the time slots are assigned to specific users and only they can transmit during that time slot.

#### Advantages and Disadvantages of TDMA

The use of time slots in TDMA has several advantages. One of the main advantages is the ability to transmit multiple users simultaneously on the same frequency band without interference. This allows for more efficient use of the limited wireless spectrum and increases the capacity of the wireless communication system. Additionally, TDMA also offers improved security, as the time slots can be used to limit the range of communication to a specific time period, making it more difficult for unauthorized users to access the network.

However, TDMA also has some disadvantages. One of the main disadvantages is the susceptibility to interference from other users. As multiple users are accessing the same frequency band, interference can occur if the time slots are not properly assigned or if there are too many users accessing the same frequency band. Additionally, TDMA also requires more complex signal processing techniques compared to FDMA and CDMA, making it more difficult to implement in practice.

### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored the principles of Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA) in wireless communication systems. TDMA is a multiple access technique that divides the time into multiple time slots, allowing multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously. While TDMA has its advantages, it also has some disadvantages that must be considered when implementing it in practice. In the next section, we will explore another multiple access technique, Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA), and its principles.





### Subsection: 3.2a TDMA Frame Structure

The TDMA frame structure is a crucial aspect of TDMA multiple access techniques. It defines the structure of the time slots and how they are assigned to different users. The frame structure is typically defined by the network operator and can vary depending on the specific network and its requirements.

#### TDMA Frame Structure

The TDMA frame structure is typically divided into multiple time slots, each assigned to a different user. The number of time slots and their duration can vary depending on the specific network and its requirements. For example, in a cellular network, the frame structure may be divided into multiple time slots, each assigned to a different user. This allows for multiple users to access the same frequency band simultaneously without interfering with each other.

The frame structure also includes synchronization information, such as preamble and postamble, to help users synchronize their transmissions. This is important for ensuring reliable communication between users.

#### TDMA Frame Structure in TDMoIP

In TDMoIP, the TDMA frame structure is used to transport multiple bit streams over a single physical link. The frame structure is defined by the TDMoIP protocol and includes a preamble, postamble, and data field. The preamble and postamble are used for synchronization and error detection, while the data field is used to carry the actual bit streams.

The TDMA frame structure in TDMoIP is also used to handle the TDM structure. This means that the frame rate is locked to the sampling frequency of voice traffic, with 8000 frames per second for T1 frames and 256 frames per second for E1 frames. This allows for efficient transport of voice traffic over the network.

#### TDMA Frame Structure in GSM Cellular Networks

In GSM cellular networks, the TDMA frame structure is used to define the structure of the Abis channel, which connects the Base Transceiver Station (BTS) and Base Station Controller (BSC). The frame structure is defined by the GSM standard and includes a basic superframe duration of 20 milliseconds. This allows for efficient transport of channel associated signalling bits, which are updated once per multiframe of 16 frames (every 2 milliseconds) for E1 trunks and once per superframe of 24 frames (3 milliseconds) for T1 ESF trunks.

#### Conclusion

The TDMA frame structure is a crucial aspect of TDMA multiple access techniques. It defines the structure of the time slots and how they are assigned to different users. The frame structure can vary depending on the specific network and its requirements, but it is essential for ensuring reliable and efficient communication between users. 





### Subsection: 3.2b TDMA Slot Allocation

TDMA slot allocation is a crucial aspect of TDMA multiple access techniques. It determines how the available time slots are assigned to different users. The slot allocation scheme can greatly impact the efficiency and fairness of the system.

#### TDMA Slot Allocation Schemes

There are several different TDMA slot allocation schemes that can be used in a TDMA system. These include:

- **Fixed Assignment:** In this scheme, each user is assigned a fixed set of time slots. This scheme is simple and easy to implement, but it may not be efficient if the traffic patterns of the users change over time.

- **Dynamic Assignment:** In this scheme, the time slots are assigned dynamically based on the current traffic conditions. This allows for more efficient use of the available slots, but it also requires more complex algorithms and signaling.

- **Demand Assignment:** In this scheme, the time slots are assigned based on the demand of the users. This can be done using a reservation-based scheme or a contention-based scheme. The reservation-based scheme requires the users to reserve the slots in advance, while the contention-based scheme allows the users to compete for the slots.

#### TDMA Slot Allocation in TDMoIP

In TDMoIP, the TDMA slot allocation is handled by the TDMoIP protocol. The protocol defines the structure of the frame and the allocation of the time slots. The slot allocation is based on the number of bit streams that need to be transported over the physical link.

The TDMA slot allocation in TDMoIP is also used to handle the TDM structure. This means that the frame rate is locked to the sampling frequency of voice traffic, with 8000 frames per second for T1 frames and 256 frames per second for E1 frames. This allows for efficient transport of voice traffic over the network.

#### TDMA Slot Allocation in GSM Cellular Networks

In GSM cellular networks, the TDMA slot allocation is used to define the structure of the Abis channel, which connects the Base Transceiver Station (BTS) and Base Station Controller (BSC). The slot allocation is based on the number of users and the traffic patterns in the cell.

The TDMA slot allocation in GSM cellular networks is also used to handle the TDM structure. This means that the frame rate is locked to the sampling frequency of voice traffic, with 8000 frames per second for T1 frames and 256 frames per second for E1 frames. This allows for efficient transport of voice traffic over the network.





### Subsection: 3.2c TDMA Synchronization

TDMA synchronization is a critical aspect of TDMA multiple access techniques. It ensures that all users in a TDMA system are synchronized, i.e., they start and end their transmissions at the same time. This synchronization is necessary to avoid interference between different users and to ensure efficient use of the available time slots.

#### TDMA Synchronization Schemes

There are several different TDMA synchronization schemes that can be used in a TDMA system. These include:

- **Clock Synchronization:** In this scheme, all users are synchronized to a common clock. This can be achieved using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

- **Frame Synchronization:** In this scheme, all users are synchronized to the start of a new frame. This can be achieved using a preamble or a synchronization sequence.

- **Slot Synchronization:** In this scheme, all users are synchronized to the start of a new slot. This can be achieved using a synchronization sequence or by using a synchronization algorithm.

#### TDMA Synchronization in TDMoIP

In TDMoIP, the TDMA synchronization is handled by the TDMoIP protocol. The protocol defines the structure of the frame and the allocation of the time slots. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

The TDMA synchronization in TDMoIP is also used to handle the TDM structure. This means that the frame rate is locked to the sampling frequency of voice traffic, with 8000 frames per second for T1 frames and 256 frames per second for E1 frames. This allows for efficient transport of voice traffic over the network.

#### TDMA Synchronization in GSM Cellular Networks

In GSM cellular networks, the TDMA synchronization is used to define the structure of the Abis interface. The Abis interface is used to connect the base station and the mobile switching center. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

The TDMA synchronization in GSM cellular networks is also used to handle the TDM structure. This means that the frame rate is locked to the sampling frequency of voice traffic, with 8000 frames per second for T1 frames and 256 frames per second for E1 frames. This allows for efficient transport of voice traffic over the network.




### Subsection: 3.2d TDMA in Cellular Networks

In cellular networks, TDMA is used to allocate time slots to different users. This allows for multiple users to share the same frequency band, increasing the capacity of the network. TDMA is particularly useful in cellular networks due to the limited availability of spectrum.

#### TDMA in GSM Cellular Networks

In GSM cellular networks, TDMA is used to allocate time slots to different users. The GSM standard defines a 200 kHz carrier spacing, which is divided into eight 25 kHz time slots. Each time slot is further divided into 15 1.6 ms frames, each containing 36 bits. This allows for a total of 120 bits per time slot, which can be used to transmit 480 bits per second.

The TDMA synchronization in GSM cellular networks is handled by the GSM protocol. The protocol defines the structure of the Abis interface, which is used to connect the base station and the mobile switching center. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

#### TDMA in CDMA Cellular Networks

In CDMA cellular networks, TDMA is used in conjunction with CDMA to allocate both time slots and code slots to different users. This allows for even more efficient use of the available spectrum.

The CDMA protocol defines the structure of the Iub interface, which is used to connect the base station and the mobile switching center. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

#### TDMA in LTE Cellular Networks

In LTE cellular networks, TDMA is used to allocate time slots to different users. The LTE standard defines a 15 kHz carrier spacing, which is divided into 16 1.6 ms time slots. Each time slot is further divided into 15 1.6 ms frames, each containing 36 bits. This allows for a total of 120 bits per time slot, which can be used to transmit 480 bits per second.

The TDMA synchronization in LTE cellular networks is handled by the LTE protocol. The protocol defines the structure of the X2 interface, which is used to connect the base station and the mobile switching center. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.




### Subsection: 3.3a CDMA Code Assignment

Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA) is a multiple access technique that allows multiple users to share the same frequency band by assigning unique codes to each user. These codes are used to differentiate between different users and allow them to transmit and receive data simultaneously without interfering with each other.

#### CDMA Code Assignment in CDMA Cellular Networks

In CDMA cellular networks, CDMA codes are assigned to each user based on their location and the available spectrum. The codes are typically assigned in a way that minimizes interference between different users. This is achieved by assigning codes that have low cross-correlation values to users in close proximity.

The CDMA protocol defines the structure of the Iub interface, which is used to connect the base station and the mobile switching center. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

#### CDMA Code Assignment in LTE Cellular Networks

In LTE cellular networks, CDMA codes are assigned to each user based on their location and the available spectrum. The codes are typically assigned in a way that minimizes interference between different users. This is achieved by assigning codes that have low cross-correlation values to users in close proximity.

The LTE standard defines a 15 kHz carrier spacing, which is divided into 16 1.6 ms time slots. Each time slot is further divided into 15 1.6 ms frames, each containing 36 bits. This allows for a total of 120 bits per time slot, which can be used to transmit 480 bits per second.

The CDMA protocol defines the structure of the Iub interface, which is used to connect the base station and the mobile switching center. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

#### CDMA Code Assignment in Satellite Communications

In satellite communications, CDMA codes are assigned to each user based on their location and the available spectrum. The codes are typically assigned in a way that minimizes interference between different users. This is achieved by assigning codes that have low cross-correlation values to users in close proximity.

The CDMA protocol defines the structure of the Iub interface, which is used to connect the satellite and the ground station. The synchronization is achieved by using a common reference signal or by using a synchronization algorithm.

### Subsection: 3.3b CDMA Transmitter and Receiver

In CDMA systems, the transmitter and receiver play a crucial role in the successful transmission and reception of data. The transmitter is responsible for converting the digital data into analog signals, modulating them onto the assigned CDMA codes, and then transmitting them over the air. The receiver, on the other hand, is responsible for receiving the transmitted signals, demodulating them, and decoding them to retrieve the original digital data.

#### CDMA Transmitter

The CDMA transmitter consists of three main components: the digital modulator, the CDMA modulator, and the RF modulator. The digital modulator is responsible for converting the digital data into analog signals. This is typically achieved using a pulse amplitude modulation (PAM) or pulse code modulation (PCM) scheme.

The CDMA modulator is responsible for modulating the analog signals onto the assigned CDMA codes. This is achieved by multiplying the analog signals with the assigned CDMA codes. The resulting signals are then passed to the RF modulator.

The RF modulator is responsible for converting the modulated analog signals into RF signals. This is typically achieved using a frequency modulation (FM) or amplitude modulation (AM) scheme. The RF signals are then transmitted over the air.

#### CDMA Receiver

The CDMA receiver consists of three main components: the RF demodulator, the CDMA demodulator, and the digital demodulator. The RF demodulator is responsible for demodulating the received RF signals. This is typically achieved using a frequency demodulation (FM) or amplitude demodulation (AM) scheme.

The CDMA demodulator is responsible for demodulating the received signals from the assigned CDMA codes. This is achieved by multiplying the received signals with the assigned CDMA codes. The resulting signals are then passed to the digital demodulator.

The digital demodulator is responsible for converting the demodulated analog signals into digital data. This is typically achieved using a pulse amplitude demodulation (PAM) or pulse code demodulation (PCM) scheme. The digital data is then decoded to retrieve the original data.

In conclusion, the CDMA transmitter and receiver play a crucial role in the successful transmission and reception of data in CDMA systems. The transmitter is responsible for converting the digital data into analog signals, modulating them onto the assigned CDMA codes, and transmitting them over the air. The receiver, on the other hand, is responsible for receiving the transmitted signals, demodulating them, and decoding them to retrieve the original digital data. 





### Subsection: 3.3b CDMA Interference Management

Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA) is a multiple access technique that allows multiple users to share the same frequency band by assigning unique codes to each user. However, due to the nature of CDMA, interference between different users is inevitable. In this section, we will discuss the various techniques used to manage interference in CDMA systems.

#### Interference Management in CDMA Cellular Networks

In CDMA cellular networks, interference management is crucial to ensure the quality of service for all users. The interference can be categorized into two types: intra-cell interference and inter-cell interference.

Intra-cell interference occurs when multiple users within the same cell transmit and receive data simultaneously. This can be managed by assigning unique codes to each user and using spread spectrum techniques to spread the signal over a wide frequency band. This allows the receiver to distinguish the desired signal from the interference.

Inter-cell interference occurs when users in adjacent cells transmit and receive data simultaneously. This can be managed by using power control techniques to adjust the transmit power of each user. This ensures that users in adjacent cells do not interfere with each other.

#### Interference Management in LTE Cellular Networks

In LTE cellular networks, interference management is achieved through the use of Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM) and Multiple Input Multiple Output (MIMO) techniques. OFDM allows for the transmission of multiple data streams simultaneously, reducing the impact of interference. MIMO, on the other hand, uses multiple antennas at both the transmitter and receiver to improve the signal quality and reduce interference.

#### Interference Management in Satellite Communications

In satellite communications, interference management is crucial to ensure the quality of service for all users. The interference can be categorized into two types: intra-satellite interference and inter-satellite interference.

Intra-satellite interference occurs when multiple users within the same satellite transmit and receive data simultaneously. This can be managed by assigning unique codes to each user and using spread spectrum techniques to spread the signal over a wide frequency band. This allows the receiver to distinguish the desired signal from the interference.

Inter-satellite interference occurs when users in adjacent satellites transmit and receive data simultaneously. This can be managed by using power control techniques to adjust the transmit power of each user. This ensures that users in adjacent satellites do not interfere with each other.

In conclusion, interference management is a crucial aspect of CDMA systems. By using various techniques such as code assignment, power control, and spread spectrum, interference can be effectively managed, ensuring the quality of service for all users. 





### Subsection: 3.3c CDMA in Cellular Networks

Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA) has been widely adopted in cellular networks due to its ability to support a large number of users in a single frequency band. In this section, we will discuss the use of CDMA in cellular networks, specifically focusing on the IS-95 standard.

#### IS-95 and CDMA

IS-95, also known as CDMAOne, is a cellular network standard that uses CDMA for multiple access. It was first introduced in the 1990s and has since been widely adopted in many parts of the world. IS-95 uses CDMA for both the uplink and downlink, allowing for efficient use of the available spectrum.

#### Block Interleaver

In IS-95, symbols are first convolution coded and then repeated before being sent to a block interleaver. The block interleaver is a 24 by 16 array, with each element representing a symbol. This interleaver helps to spread out the symbols in time, reducing the impact of fading and improving the overall reliability of the system.

#### Capacity

The capacity of IS-95, like any other communications system, is limited according to Shannon's theorem. However, IS-95 fares well in the digital world due to its use of CDMA and other techniques to improve the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR). With CDMA, signals that are not correlated with the channel of interest appear as noise, while signals carried on other Walsh codes that are properly time aligned are essentially removed in the de-spreading process. This allows IS-95 to squeeze more users into the same radio spectrum.

Active power control is also used in IS-95, where the network controls the transmitted power of the traffic channel to keep the signal quality just good enough, thereby keeping the noise level seen by all other users to a minimum. This is achieved through the use of the receiver's techniques, such as the rake receiver and soft handoff.

#### Layer 2

Once a call is established, a mobile is restricted to using the traffic channel. A frame format is defined in the Media Access Control (MAC) for the traffic channel that allows the regular voice or data bits to be multiplexed with signaling message fragments. The signaling message fragments are pieced together in the Lower Layer 3 (LL3) to form complete signaling messages. This allows for efficient use of the available bandwidth and reduces the overhead of signaling messages.

In conclusion, CDMA has been a crucial component of cellular networks, allowing for efficient use of the available spectrum and supporting a large number of users. Its use in IS-95 has been particularly successful, demonstrating the power and versatility of CDMA in modern communication systems.





### Subsection: 3.3d CDMA vs. FDMA and TDMA

Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA) is a multiple access technique that allows multiple users to share the same frequency band simultaneously. It is a form of spread spectrum communication, where each user's signal is spread over a wide frequency band. This allows for efficient use of the available spectrum, as multiple users can share the same band without interfering with each other.

CDMA has several advantages over other multiple access techniques, such as Frequency Division Multiple Access (FDMA) and Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA). In this section, we will discuss these advantages and how they make CDMA a popular choice in modern wireless communication systems.

#### Efficient Practical Utilization of the Fixed Frequency Spectrum

In theory, CDMA, TDMA, and FDMA have exactly the same spectral efficiency. However, in practice, each technique has its own challenges that can limit its efficiency. For example, TDMA systems must carefully synchronize the transmission times of all users to ensure that they are received in the correct time slot and do not cause interference. This can be challenging in a mobile environment, where user mobility can cause unpredictable changes in the signal's delay and Doppler shift. As a result, each time slot must have a guard time, which reduces the probability that users will interfere but decreases the spectral efficiency.

Similarly, FDMA systems must use a guard band between adjacent channels, due to the unpredictable Doppler shift of the signal spectrum because of user mobility. The guard bands will reduce the probability that adjacent channels will interfere, but decrease the utilization of the spectrum.

In contrast, CDMA systems do not require precise synchronization or guard bands. This makes them more efficient in practical use, especially in mobile environments where user mobility can cause unpredictable changes in the signal's delay and Doppler shift.

#### Flexible Allocation of Resources

Another key advantage of CDMA is its flexible allocation of resources. In the case of CDM (synchronous CDMA), TDMA, and FDMA, the number of simultaneous orthogonal codes, time slots, and frequency slots respectively are fixed. This limits the capacity of these systems, as there are a fixed number of orthogonal codes, time slots, or frequency bands that can be allocated for CDM, TDMA, and FDMA systems. However, in an asynchronous CDMA system, the number of users that can be supported is only limited by the desired bit error probability. This is because the Signal-to-Interference Ratio (SIR) varies inversely with the number of users, and a larger number of users can be supported as long as the SIR remains above the desired level. This makes CDMA more scalable and adaptable to changing traffic conditions.

In conclusion, CDMA offers several advantages over other multiple access techniques, making it a popular choice in modern wireless communication systems. Its efficient use of the fixed frequency spectrum and flexible allocation of resources make it a versatile and adaptable solution for wireless communication.





### Subsection: 3.4a OFDMA Subcarrier Allocation

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access (OFDMA) is a multiple access technique that allows multiple users to share the same frequency band simultaneously. It is a form of spread spectrum communication, where each user's signal is spread over a wide frequency band. This allows for efficient use of the available spectrum, as multiple users can share the same band without interfering with each other.

In OFDMA, the available spectrum is divided into multiple subcarriers, each of which is assigned to a different user. This allows for the simultaneous transmission of multiple signals, each with its own subcarrier. The subcarriers are carefully designed to be orthogonal to each other, meaning that they do not interfere with each other. This is achieved by assigning each subcarrier a unique set of frequencies that are orthogonal to the frequencies of the other subcarriers.

The allocation of subcarriers to users is a critical aspect of OFDMA. It determines how the available spectrum is divided among the users and how much bandwidth each user has. The allocation can be static, where each user is assigned a fixed set of subcarriers, or dynamic, where the allocation is changed based on the current channel conditions.

Dynamic subcarrier allocation is particularly useful in OFDMA, as it allows for adaptive user-to-subcarrier assignment. This can be achieved based on feedback information about the channel conditions. If the assignment is done sufficiently fast, it can further improve the OFDMA robustness to fast fading and narrow-band co-channel interference, and make it possible to achieve even better system spectral efficiency.

Different numbers of sub-carriers can be assigned to different users, in view to support differentiated quality of service (QoS), i.e. to control the data rate and error probability individually for each user. This allows for a more flexible allocation of resources, as users with different data rate requirements can be assigned different numbers of subcarriers.

OFDMA can be seen as an alternative to combining OFDM with time-division multiple access (TDMA) or time-domain statistical multiplexing communication. Low-data-rate users can send continuously with low transmission power instead of using a "pulsed" high-power carrier. Constant delay, and shorter delay, can be achieved.

OFDMA can also be described as a combination of frequency-domain and time-domain multiple access, where the resources are partitioned in the timeâ€“frequency space, and slots are assigned along the OFDM symbol index, as well as OFDM sub-carrier index. This allows for a more flexible and efficient use of the available spectrum.

In conclusion, OFDMA subcarrier allocation is a critical aspect of OFDMA that determines how the available spectrum is divided among the users and how much bandwidth each user has. Dynamic subcarrier allocation, based on feedback information about the channel conditions, can further improve the OFDMA robustness and spectral efficiency.





### Subsection: 3.4b OFDMA in 4G and 5G Networks

The evolution of wireless communication systems has been marked by the introduction of new technologies and standards, each aimed at improving performance and efficiency. The 4th Generation (4G) and 5th Generation (5G) of wireless communication systems are the latest in this evolution, and they have brought with them significant advancements in multiple access techniques.

#### 4G Wireless Communication Systems

The 4G wireless communication systems, also known as Long Term Evolution (LTE), have been designed to provide higher data rates, lower latency, and improved spectral efficiency compared to their 3G predecessors. One of the key technologies that have enabled these improvements is Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access (OFDMA).

In 4G LTE systems, OFDMA is used in the downlink to divide the available spectrum into multiple subcarriers, each of which is assigned to a different user. This allows for the simultaneous transmission of multiple signals, each with its own subcarrier. The use of OFDMA in 4G LTE systems has significantly improved spectral efficiency, allowing for higher data rates and improved quality of service.

#### 5G Wireless Communication Systems

The 5G wireless communication systems, currently in the early stages of deployment, are expected to provide even greater improvements in data rates, latency, and spectral efficiency. One of the key technologies that will enable these improvements is Multi-Carrier Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access (MC-OFDMA).

MC-OFDMA is an extension of OFDMA that allows for the aggregation of more than two carriers. This is particularly useful in 5G systems, where the available spectrum is often fragmented and non-contiguous. By aggregating multiple carriers, MC-OFDMA can provide higher data rates and improved spectral efficiency, even in the presence of non-contiguous spectrum.

The IEEE 802.11ah standard, for example, specifies the use of MC-OFDMA in the 5 GHz band. This allows for the aggregation of up to four carriers, each with a bandwidth of 20 MHz, for a total bandwidth of 80 MHz. This is expected to provide peak transfer rates of up to 672 Mbit/s, a significant improvement over the 168 Mbit/s and 22 Mbit/s peak speeds achievable with single-carrier HSPA+.

In conclusion, OFDMA has played a crucial role in the evolution of wireless communication systems, enabling significant improvements in data rates, latency, and spectral efficiency. With the introduction of MC-OFDMA in 5G systems, we can expect to see even greater improvements in the future.




### Subsection: 3.4c OFDMA vs. CDMA and TDMA

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access (OFDMA) is a multiple access technique that is used in wireless communication systems to allow multiple users to share the same frequency band. It is a form of frequency division multiple access (FDMA) where multiple users are assigned different subcarriers within the same frequency band. OFDMA is used in various wireless communication standards, including Wi-Fi, LTE, and 5G.

#### OFDMA vs. CDMA

Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA) is another multiple access technique that is used in wireless communication systems. Unlike OFDMA, which divides the frequency band into multiple subcarriers, CDMA divides the time into multiple time slots and assigns each user a unique code. This allows multiple users to share the same frequency band without interfering with each other.

One of the key advantages of OFDMA over CDMA is its ability to provide more efficient use of the available spectrum. In CDMA, the time slots are fixed and cannot be dynamically allocated, which can lead to underutilization of the spectrum. In contrast, OFDMA allows for the dynamic allocation of subcarriers, which can be adjusted based on the traffic load and the specific needs of each user.

Another advantage of OFDMA is its ability to provide better spectral efficiency. In CDMA, the users are assigned unique codes, which can lead to interference between users with similar codes. This interference can degrade the performance of the system. In OFDMA, the users are assigned different subcarriers, which are orthogonal to each other. This eliminates the interference between users and allows for more efficient use of the spectrum.

#### OFDMA vs. TDMA

Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA) is another multiple access technique that is used in wireless communication systems. Similar to CDMA, TDMA divides the time into multiple time slots and assigns each user a unique time slot. However, unlike CDMA, TDMA does not use unique codes for each user.

One of the key advantages of OFDMA over TDMA is its ability to provide more efficient use of the available spectrum. In TDMA, the time slots are fixed and cannot be dynamically allocated, which can lead to underutilization of the spectrum. In contrast, OFDMA allows for the dynamic allocation of subcarriers, which can be adjusted based on the traffic load and the specific needs of each user.

Another advantage of OFDMA is its ability to provide better spectral efficiency. In TDMA, the users are assigned unique time slots, which can lead to interference between users with adjacent time slots. This interference can degrade the performance of the system. In OFDMA, the users are assigned different subcarriers, which are orthogonal to each other. This eliminates the interference between users and allows for more efficient use of the spectrum.

In conclusion, OFDMA offers several advantages over CDMA and TDMA, including more efficient use of the available spectrum and better spectral efficiency. These advantages make OFDMA a popular multiple access technique in modern wireless communication systems.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the various multiple access techniques used in wireless communications. We have discussed the advantages and disadvantages of each technique, and how they are used in different scenarios. We have also looked at the mathematical models behind these techniques, and how they can be applied in practical situations.

We began by discussing the concept of multiple access and its importance in wireless communications. We then delved into the different types of multiple access techniques, including frequency division multiple access (FDMA), time division multiple access (TDMA), and code division multiple access (CDMA). We also explored the concept of spread spectrum and its role in multiple access.

Furthermore, we discussed the challenges faced in implementing these techniques, such as interference and synchronization issues. We also looked at how these challenges can be mitigated through various techniques, such as equalization and error correction coding.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of multiple access techniques and their role in wireless communications. It is our hope that this knowledge will serve as a solid foundation for further exploration and understanding of wireless communications.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of wireless networks and how they play a crucial role in our modern society.

Wireless networks are a collection of wireless devices that communicate with each other to form a network. These networks can be used for a variety of purposes, such as voice communication, data transmission, and internet access. The key advantage of wireless networks is their ability to provide mobility and flexibility, making them an essential component of modern communication systems.

In this chapter, we will delve into the various aspects of wireless networks, including their architecture, protocols, and applications. We will also discuss the challenges and limitations of wireless networks and how they can be overcome. By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of wireless networks and their role in wireless communication. ## Chapter 4: Wireless Networks:




### Subsection: 3.4d OFDMA Synchronization

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiple Access (OFDMA) is a multiple access technique that allows multiple users to share the same frequency band. In order for OFDMA to work effectively, it is crucial for the users to be synchronized. This means that all users must have the same timing and frequency information. In this section, we will discuss the synchronization process in OFDMA.

#### OFDMA Synchronization Process

The synchronization process in OFDMA involves three main steps: timing synchronization, frequency synchronization, and symbol synchronization. Timing synchronization refers to the alignment of the timing of different users. This is necessary because in OFDMA, different users may have different transmission times. Frequency synchronization refers to the alignment of the frequency of different users. This is necessary because in OFDMA, different users may have different subcarriers. Symbol synchronization refers to the alignment of the symbol boundaries of different users. This is necessary because in OFDMA, different users may have different symbol durations.

#### Timing Synchronization

Timing synchronization in OFDMA is achieved through the use of a common reference signal. This signal is transmitted by a synchronization source and is used by all users to align their timing. The synchronization source can be a centralized entity or a distributed entity. In a centralized system, the synchronization source is a dedicated node that transmits the reference signal to all users. In a distributed system, the synchronization source is a user that transmits the reference signal to other users.

#### Frequency Synchronization

Frequency synchronization in OFDMA is achieved through the use of a common reference signal. This signal is transmitted by a synchronization source and is used by all users to align their frequency. The synchronization source can be a centralized entity or a distributed entity. In a centralized system, the synchronization source is a dedicated node that transmits the reference signal to all users. In a distributed system, the synchronization source is a user that transmits the reference signal to other users.

#### Symbol Synchronization

Symbol synchronization in OFDMA is achieved through the use of a common reference signal. This signal is transmitted by a synchronization source and is used by all users to align their symbol boundaries. The synchronization source can be a centralized entity or a distributed entity. In a centralized system, the synchronization source is a dedicated node that transmits the reference signal to all users. In a distributed system, the synchronization source is a user that transmits the reference signal to other users.

#### Conclusion

In conclusion, OFDMA synchronization is a crucial aspect of OFDMA systems. It allows for efficient use of the available spectrum and improves the overall performance of the system. The synchronization process involves timing synchronization, frequency synchronization, and symbol synchronization, which are all achieved through the use of a common reference signal. The synchronization source can be a centralized entity or a distributed entity, depending on the system design. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the various multiple access techniques used in wireless communications. These techniques are essential for efficient use of the limited spectrum resources and for enabling multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. We have discussed the two main categories of multiple access techniques: frequency division multiple access (FDMA) and time division multiple access (TDMA). We have also delved into the more modern code division multiple access (CDMA) and space division multiple access (SDMA) techniques. Each of these techniques has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of which one to use depends on the specific requirements of the wireless communication system.

FDMA is a simple and straightforward technique that divides the available frequency spectrum into multiple channels, each of which is assigned to a different user. This allows multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously without interfering with each other. However, FDMA is not very efficient in terms of spectrum utilization, as each channel is assigned to a single user.

TDMA, on the other hand, divides the available time into multiple time slots, each of which is assigned to a different user. This allows multiple users to access the same wireless channel sequentially, with each user being assigned a different time slot. TDMA is more efficient in terms of spectrum utilization than FDMA, as multiple users can share the same frequency band. However, it is more complex to implement and requires synchronization between users.

CDMA is a more modern technique that uses spread spectrum signals to enable multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. This is achieved by assigning a unique code to each user, which is used to spread and de-spread the signals. CDMA is highly efficient in terms of spectrum utilization, as multiple users can share the same frequency band without interfering with each other. However, it requires complex signal processing techniques and is susceptible to interference from other sources.

SDMA is a technique that uses multiple antennas at the receiver to separate the signals from different users. This allows multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously without interfering with each other. SDMA is highly efficient in terms of spectrum utilization, as it can support a large number of users with a limited number of antennas. However, it requires complex signal processing techniques and is susceptible to interference from other sources.

In conclusion, multiple access techniques play a crucial role in wireless communications by enabling multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. Each technique has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of which one to use depends on the specific requirements of the wireless communication system.

## Chapter: Principles of Wireless Communications: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In today's world, wireless communication has become an integral part of our daily lives. From simple phone calls to complex data transmissions, wireless communication has revolutionized the way we interact and access information. As the demand for wireless communication continues to grow, so does the need for efficient and reliable wireless networks. In this chapter, we will explore the principles of wireless networks, which form the backbone of modern wireless communication systems.

Wireless networks are a collection of interconnected devices that communicate wirelessly, without the need for physical cables. These networks are used in a wide range of applications, from personal communication devices to large-scale enterprise networks. The design and implementation of wireless networks require a deep understanding of the underlying principles and techniques.

In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to wireless networks, including network topologies, protocols, and security. We will also discuss the challenges and solutions associated with wireless networks, such as interference, signal strength, and power management. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of wireless networks and be able to design and implement efficient and reliable wireless communication systems.

Whether you are a student, researcher, or industry professional, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for understanding the principles of wireless networks. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of wireless communication.


## Chapter 4: Wireless Networks:




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the various multiple access techniques used in wireless communications. These techniques are essential for efficient use of the limited spectrum resources and for enabling multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. We have discussed the two main categories of multiple access techniques: frequency division multiple access (FDMA) and time division multiple access (TDMA). We have also delved into the more modern code division multiple access (CDMA) and space division multiple access (SDMA) techniques. Each of these techniques has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of which one to use depends on the specific requirements of the wireless communication system.

FDMA is a simple and straightforward technique that divides the available frequency spectrum into multiple channels, each of which is assigned to a different user. This allows multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously without interfering with each other. However, FDMA is not very efficient in terms of spectrum utilization, as each channel is assigned to a single user.

TDMA, on the other hand, divides the available time into multiple time slots, each of which is assigned to a different user. This allows multiple users to access the same wireless channel sequentially, with each user being assigned a different time slot. TDMA is more efficient in terms of spectrum utilization than FDMA, as multiple users can share the same frequency band. However, it is more complex to implement and requires synchronization between users.

CDMA is a more modern technique that uses spread spectrum signals to enable multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. This is achieved by assigning a unique code to each user, which is used to spread and de-spread the signals. CDMA is highly efficient in terms of spectrum utilization, as multiple users can share the same frequency band without interfering with each other. However, it requires complex signal processing techniques and is susceptible to interference from other sources.

SDMA is a technique that uses multiple antennas at the receiver to separate the signals from different users. This allows multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously without interfering with each other. SDMA is highly efficient in terms of spectrum utilization, as it can support a large number of users with a limited number of antennas. However, it requires complex signal processing techniques and is susceptible to interference from other sources.

In conclusion, multiple access techniques play a crucial role in wireless communications by enabling multiple users to access the same wireless channel simultaneously. Each technique has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of which one to use depends on the specific requirements of the wireless communication system.

As the demand for wireless communication continues to grow, so does the need for efficient and reliable wireless networks. In this chapter, we will explore the principles of wireless networks, which form the backbone of modern wireless communication systems.

Wireless networks are a collection of interconnected devices that communicate wirelessly, without the need for physical cables. These networks are used in a wide range of applications, from personal communication devices to large-scale enterprise networks. The design and implementation of wireless networks require a deep understanding of the underlying principles and techniques.

In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to wireless networks, including network topologies, protocols, and security. We will also discuss the challenges and solutions associated with wireless networks, such as interference, signal strength, and power management. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of wireless networks and be able to design and implement efficient and reliable wireless communication systems.
